  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_806306369" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/806306369" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12248943-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499990463p2/12248943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/806306369?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/12248943-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12248943-mike?shelf=realistic-fiction">realistic-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer806306369" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9260611453930917239">This book uses one of my favorite structures for a novel: it divides its time almost evenly between a story set in the present day and flashbacks telling a story set in the past. It's a great way to avoid infodumps when a story requires a lot of background - we get all of the background, and it's a lot more interesting than just telling us everything in a couple paragraphs. When done really well, it can also add mystery into the novel, and it lends subtle connections between the story in the pas</span>
  <span id="freeText9260611453930917239" style="display:none">This book uses one of my favorite structures for a novel: it divides its time almost evenly between a story set in the present day and flashbacks telling a story set in the past. It's a great way to avoid infodumps when a story requires a lot of background - we get all of the background, and it's a lot more interesting than just telling us everything in a couple paragraphs. When done really well, it can also add mystery into the novel, and it lends subtle connections between the story in the past and the one in the present day. Patrick Ness' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18285151.More_Than_This__More_Than___1_" title="More Than This (More Than, #1) by Jay McLean" rel="nofollow noopener"> More Than This</a> and Justine Larbalestier's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15957.Patron_Saint_of_Liars" title="Patron Saint of Liars by Ann Patchett" rel="nofollow noopener"> Liar</a> both use the structure to their advantage, with both books becoming more subtle and surprising because of it. It can be used badly, sure - just look at Lenore Appelhans' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12614754.The_Memory_of_After__The_Memory_Chronicles___1_" title="The Memory of After (The Memory Chronicles, #1) by Lenore Appelhans" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Memory of After</a>. But when it's used well, the story set in the present and the one set in the past are essential to each other, so intrinsically tied to each other that it doesn't even occur to you to decide if one is more entertaining than the other. The two plots each in More Than This and Liar don't feel awkwardly shoved together; they're put together because they wouldn't be complete alone.<br /><br />I can't say that's the case here. Here's the premise of the book: when Arlene was a teenager, she lived in Alabama (hence the title), and she did something so awful that when she graduated high school, she moved to Chicago and swore to God that she'd never go back to Alabama. (We find out what she did that was so awful in the flashback-story). Ten years or so later, she and her boyfriend are finally going back to Alabama, mostly because of how much Arlene's Aunt Florence begged her.<br /><br />The problem is that Arlene's teen years are so much more interesting than the present-day story. Maybe this is just the YA fan that I am talking, but the flashback plot was just so much more thematically interesting and intense than present-day plot. The flashback plot is an honest portrayal of intense jealousy, to the extent of self-hatred, and the extreme measures that people take when they're that jealous. Arlene, in these sections was a very unique character (although the nature of the flashbacks kept us at an arm's length from her), and I genuinely can't think of a single novel that explores jealousy and isolation the way these sections did. On its own, these sections could've been pulled together to make one of the most unique novels I've read in a while.<br /><br />The sections set in the present-day, by contrast, really didn't have very much to say, apart from 'southerners are racist'. Arlene is a much less interesting character in these sections, and there's no real plot. When characters from the flashback-sections showed up, it always felt like a bad sequel, over-the-top caricatures of the characters that seemed like genuine people in the flashbacks. Take Rose Mallory, for example, the pretty and popular girl that Arlene was jealous of in the flashback-plots. We don't see a ton of her, but it's implied that her happiness is largely an act; she's abused by her father, and probably her boyfriend. This isn't exactly subtle or groundbreaking, but it's at least realistic, and it plays into the themes of this section well. When she shows up in the present day story, she's an intense man-hater. She honestly serves more as comic relief than as any legitimate role in Arlene's story, routinely attacking Burr (Arlene's boyfriend) due to misunderstandings. It's not funny or entertaining, and it undermines the weight that the flashback sections had. It's harder to take Rose Mallory's pain seriously when the results of her pain are shown to be so over-the-top.<br /><br />But the problems with the present day sections go deeper than my conviction that it's not as interesting as the flashback sections. The much bigger problem is that it's not necessary. The present day sections are unfocused, with no real plot except for adding onto the flashback sections. They're so dependent on the flashback sections that at times, they came across like a very boring commentary. When there are two related but distinct stories like this, they need to be dependent on each other, in a thematic sense if not a plot one. The flashback sections gain nothing thematically from the present day sections, and the present day sections derive all of their themes from the flashback sections. With the more interesting section working fine on its own, I have to wonder why the less interesting section was told at all. It only served to make the novel harder to get through, and it made it harder for me to appreciate the good writing and characterization on display.<br /><br />The other big problem that this book has is one that also plagued another one of Jackson's books, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1266297.The_Girl_Who_Stopped_Swimming" title="The Girl Who Stopped Swimming by Joshilyn Jackson" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Girl Who Stopped Swimming</a>. Jackson has a lot of trouble keeping her writing within the bounds of realism. But whereas The Girl Who Stopped Swimming kind of tries to trick you into thinking it might be realistic until the climax, this novel at least admits from the get-go that it's not going to be realistic. The lack of realism starts when we find out why Arlene moved to Alabama. I won't spoil it for you, but I'll just say that it reminded me of the climax of a novel with too many twists for you to process them all, and you're just left with a vague and confusing numbness. It was a string of several unlikely events all in a row, without any indication that Arlene, or even Jackson, was aware of how unlikely they were. But the biggest problem, as far as lack of realism goes, was Aunt Florence's character in the present-day sections. She's <i>way</i> over-the-top with her nastiness and racism. Her dialogue consists largely of forced snark, as if Jackson's editor told her that she had to be angry at Arlene in some way in <i>every single line of dialogue</i>. It got tiresome after a while, and it blatantly telegraphed Aunt Florence's eventual redemption. Like most of the characters in the present day sections (except Burr, who I actually kind of liked), Aunt Florence was too over-the-top to get any sort of sympathy from me, even after her redemption.<br /><br />I know most of this review was negative, but there really is a lot to like here, especially towards the end of the flashback sections. The flashbacks really are engaging, and as you're reading them, if you can forget about the present day sections, you might have a really good time. But then, alas, the present day sections come back to ruin everything. It's a mixed bag, is what I'm saying; if you have to read a Joshilyn Jackson book, I'd recommend <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17349119.Someone_Else_s_Love_Story" title="Someone Else's Love Story by Joshilyn Jackson" rel="nofollow noopener"> Someone Else's Love Story</a> over this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9260611453930917239" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8Op3fArgV1+kD/pJm4EDS3t92TI31PNf2VbQmQCD5vDTzG/bPBR2C3mqxtKZjRpZbPEw3SzvcKzit1LB2xbI6A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('82swHrLLAd7n99FGPK7xChBeeoaCCZS0MWFIky4Q0+jQTSi5hD8gijpS7d0+ougYB9KTaZkyF0cKgMrL9YX98A==')})
  }

Her face has become one of the most recognizable images in the world. She was an independent woman and thinker centuries before her time. But who was Egypt\'s Queen Nefertiti?\n\nAfter years of intense research,<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText14457830171755896964\" style=\"display:none\">Her power was rivaled only by her beauty. Her face has become one of the most recognizable images in the world. She was an independent woman and thinker centuries before her time. But who was Egypt\'s Queen Nefertiti?\n\nAfter years of intense research, Dr. Joann Fletcher has answered the questions countless researchers before her could not. While studying Egyptian royal wigs, she read a brief mention of an unidentified and mummified body, discovered long ago and believed to belong to an Egyptian of little importance. This body happened to have a wig, which Dr. Fletcher knew was a clear sign of power. After examining the hairpiece and the woman to which it belonged, to the astonishment of her colleagues she identified this body as the missing remains of Queen Nefertiti.\n\nThe search for Nefertiti had ended. She had been found. But the questions were just beginning.\n\nNefertiti first rose to prominence in Egyptology in 1912, when a three-thousand-year-old bust of the queen was unearthed and quickly became a recognizable artifact around the world. But pieces of Nefertiti\'s life remained missing. The world had seen what she looked like, but few knew about her place in history.\n\nVirtually nothing is recorded about Nefertiti\'s early years. What is known about her life starts with her rise to power, her breaking through the sex barrier to rule as a virtual co-Pharaoh alongside her husband, Akhenaten. Upon his death she took full control of his kingdom. The Egyptian people loved her and celebrated her beauty in art, but the priests did not feel the same way. They believed Nefertiti\'s power over her husband was so great that she would instill her monotheistic beliefs upon him, rendering their own power obsolete. Egyptologists concur that it was these priests who, upon Nefertiti\'s death, had her name erased from public record and any likeness of her defaced. This ultimately led to her being left out of history for three thousand years.\n\nIn The Search for Nefertiti Dr. Fletcher, an esteemed Egyptologist, traces not only her thirteen-year search for this woman, whose beauty was as great as her power, but also brings to the forefront the way Egypt\'s royal dead have been treated over time by people as varied as Agatha Christie and Adolf Hitler. She also explores how modern technology and forensics are quickly changing the field of archaeology and, in turn, what we know about history.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"14457830171755896964\" Surprised by her own ingenuity yet unused to the intense feelings of protectiveness she feels for them, Lillian ultimately begins to accept that she needs these strange children as much as they need her—urgently and fiercely. Couldn’t this be the start of the amazing life she’d always hoped for?\n\nWith white-hot wit and a big, tender heart, Kevin Wilson has written his best book yet—a most unusual story of parental love.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"14622390871607314546\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer <span id="freeTextContainer7593821120066655160">Jackson's latest novel, NEVER HAVE I EVER pubs July 30, 2019<br/><br/>New York Times and USA today bestselling novelist Joshilyn Jackson is the author of NEVER HAVE I EVER and eight other books, including gods in Alabama and The Almost Sisters. Her work has been translated into more than a dozen languages, won SIBA’s novel of the year, three times been a #1 Book Sense Pick, been the Target Book Club Pick, a</span>
  <span id="freeText7593821120066655160" style="display:none">Jackson's latest novel, NEVER HAVE I EVER pubs July 30, 2019<br /><br />New York Times and USA today bestselling novelist Joshilyn Jackson is the author of NEVER HAVE I EVER and eight other books, including gods in Alabama and The Almost Sisters. Her work has been translated into more than a dozen languages, won SIBA’s novel of the year, three times been a #1 Book Sense Pick, been the Target Book Club Pick, and three times been shortlisted for the Townsend prize. A former actor, Jackson reads the audio versions of her novels; her work in this field has been nominated for the Audie Award, was selected by AudioFile Magazine for their best of the year list, and garnered two Listen Up Awards from Publisher’s Weekly.<br /><br />She lives in Decatur, Georgia with her family.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
